class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><p>It’s common for cybercriminals to launch an attack, then shortly thereafter stop the campaign before they are detected. These breaks also give these bad actors a chance to change tactics to, once again, attempt to avoid detection. That’s what operators using the Emotet malware did, taking a short break before bringing Emotet back in a new, more dangerous form.</p>

<p>Emotet operators took about a two-month break as command and control (C&amp;C) servers went down in late May and came back online around the end of August. Then, we began observing a new version of this malware around mid-September.</p>

<p>Emotet started as a banking trojan in 2014. However, it has morphed into a very prominent threat. Now, it is mostly used for spamming and downloading additional malware threats on a target system. Based on the unique sample count of malware threats seen by the Zscaler Cloud Sandbox, Emotet and its downloaders appear to be among the most prevalent threats in 2019, followed by banking trojans and loaders, such as TrickBot and Ursnif, remote-access trojans (RATs), and off-the-shelf password stealers, such as LokiBot and AZORult.</p>

<p>Emotet is modular by design, as it supports multiple modules for different tasks, such as stealing information, spamming, and more. It is also known to download and to be downloaded by other malware families, such as TrickBot and Ursnif. It has also been associated with the Ryuk ransomware.<br/>
 </p>

<h2>Email conversation hijacking</h2>

<p>This year, Emotet employed a new tactic of using stolen email content in spam campaigns. The hijacking of existing email threads can be very effective as recipients are tricked into believing that the email was sent by the other person in the email thread. This trust factor can lead to the victim opening the email (and attachment) and getting infected with Emotet, effectively making the infected system part of an Emotet botnet.</p>

<p><img alt="Graph showing break in emotet activity from the beginning of June 2019 to mid september 2019" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/2%20emotet_gap.png" style="height:250px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 1: Emotet activity from the beginning of June 2019 to mid-September 2019.</em></p>

<p><img alt="Emotet new campaign after break" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/3%20emotet%20after%20break.png" style="height:250px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 2: The new Emotet campaign after the break.</em></p>

<p> </p>

<h2>New campaign, new document templates, and new botnets?</h2>

<p>We observed the following new templates in spammed malicious documents (maldocs) during this new campaign.</p>

<p><img alt="New Macro Template (Product Notice) " src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/4%20macro_template1.png" style="float:left;height:243px;width:349px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/>  <img alt="New Macro Template (Protected View) " src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/5%20macro_template3.png" style="height:243px;width:349px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figures 3 and 4: New macro templates (Product Notice and Protected View)</em><br/>
 </p>

<p>Earlier, there were two Emotet botnets, known as Epoch 1 (E1) and Epoch2 (E2), that were using unique RSA keys to communicate with their C&amp;C. After the break, we noticed three new RSA keys being used, which suggests the possibility of a botnet splitting into multiple botnets. Earlier keys were no longer seen in use and the latest three keys are now being used, which means operators are reorganizing their botnet infrastructure.</p>

<p><br/>
<strong>Already existing RSA keys </strong><br/>
-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----\nMHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhAMPLgcO0RQdJg/LTgiku57nH4KcLwHCx\nS0lbynOUhHhKjTnmENrMA2idUbK6hI0JRZtii9oJSlb3e5NZiCK+Qr/NB2u7ZNRc\nhG87aibm0ndS9xKDRXcmWwaQkF0PFuOHpwIDAQAB\n-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

<p>-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----\nMHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhAL9KRKWqcld40xbUZ6hRh+fPNkgJe7K+\n0y1rR0UFqc2SBmnyoR/2Ctd+8MRvU8zri2eNVkVBxCUH1Cthf3AEgRqY2kGva8gJ\nWcqls3j7RztZzqFoL+wM9DNnz/OWuiyPAQIDAQAB\n-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

<p><strong>New RSA keys</strong><br/>
-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----<br/>
MHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhALk+KlHgOKXm9eDkWu2yN9lanjwOm6W2\nPV0tgr4msNVby2pOJ6S1MZQnQwxl7y6WWzT4kveAQhLmW8JB2M2PDOxZOgVMJH2C\nAtkVW1p/P9jNJWVvjK9SmrbLdIeiKNtRfQIDAQAB<br/>
-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

<p>-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----<br/>
MHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhAM426uN11n2LZDk/JiS93WIWG7fGCQmP\n4h5yIJUxJwrjwtGVexCelD2WKrDw9sa/xKwmQKk3b2fUhwnHXjoSpR7pLaDo7pEc\niJB5y6hjbPyrSfL3Fxu74M2SAS0Arj3uAQIDAQAB<br/>
-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

MHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhAOzoTryw1r9RxRJPFKalO4+q7JaDZWSB\nKZlEc22H6ITuE06tvJspue42TF1yk8xN+1bqW++QeV6Clm1uRswA/qoao/6p4eN0\nh4zIO8PEaJ0C/9EO4cx9yfRLlVpjdEkP0QIDAQAB<br/>
-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

<p><img alt="Emotet RSA keys use over weeks of year" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/emotet%20rsa%20keys%20over%20weeks.png" style="height:292px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 5: Emotet RSA keys used before and after the break.</em></p>

<p><br/>
RSA1 and RSA2 were used before the break. In this new campaign, we saw Emotet using RSA3, RSA4, and RSA5. (1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 are assigned based on their first observation sequence in the wild).</p>

<p>Before the break, the two RSA keys didn&#x27;t share any C&amp;C infrastructure. In this new campaign, two sub-botnets are sharing some infrastructure (as shown in the following screenshots).</p>

<p><img alt="Old RSA keys and C&amp;C infrastructure" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/old_rsa_keys.png" style="height:371px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 6: Emotet RSA keys and C&amp;C infrastructure before the break.</em></p>

<p><em><img alt="New RSA keys and C&amp;C infrastructure" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/new_rsa_keys.png" style="height:371px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></em></p>

<p><em>Figure 7: RSA keys and C&amp;C infrastructure of the new Emotet campaign.</em></p>

<p>If we check the overall C&amp;C infrastructure and RSA key relationships before and after the break, we can clearly see a reorganization of the C&amp;C infrastructure, which is now divided among three new Epochs. One Epoch is divided into two while the other one is used to create a single botnet with some new C&amp;Cs.</p>

<p><img alt="RSA key and C&amp;C infrastrucutre share between before and after botnets" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/before_after_rsa_keys_shared_c2.png" style="height:371px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 8: The Emotet RSA key and C&amp;C infrastructure relationships before and after the break.</em><br/>
<br/>
 </p>

<h2>Emotet Downloader payload - Technical analysis</h2>

<p>The Emotet infection cycle generally starts with spam emails containing malicious macro documents that drop a JavaScript file. This JavaScript file further downloads the Emotet payload from a compromised WordPress website. Almost all the samples we observed were served from compromised WordPress websites (mostly version 5.2.3). </p>

<p>We will take a look at one such malicious document for the purpose of analysis here - <br/>
MD5 – 359696113a2156617c28d4f79cc7d44b (“file 20190924 LTR6051.doc”)</p>

<p>The macro in the documents is quite simple and straightforward but contains lots of junk.</p>

<p><img alt="Macro code containing junk instructions" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/6%20macro_with_junk.png" style="height:327px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 9: Macro code containing junk instructions.</em><br/>
 </p>

<p>After removing the junk, this is how the macro code looks.</p>

<p><img alt="Cleaned Macro code" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/7%20cleaned_macro.png" style="height:609px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 10: Cleaned macro code.</em><br/>
 </p>

<p>It gets its text from TextBox1 in UserForm2, then saves that in a &quot;JS&quot; file before executing that file.</p>

<p><img alt="User form containing javascript code" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/8%20user_form.png" style="height:216px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 11: A user form containing javascript code.</em><br/>
 </p>

<p>This JavaScript file is heavily obfuscated. More obfuscation is being added to the &quot;JS&quot; code incrementally. As in earlier versions of this downloader, some of the strings and function names were readable and now almost every string is obfuscated.</p>

<p><img alt="Heavily obfuscated script" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/9%20obfuscated_js.png" style="height:476px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 12: Heavily obfuscated script</em></p>

<p><br/>
This script contains an array of strings in variable “a.” First, the elements of the array are shuffled using an anonymous function just after the array definition. Then there is function “b,” which is used to decrypt strings and is extensively used throughout the script. Using this function, we can log the decrypted strings just before they return. Some of the interesting strings include:</p>

<ul>
	<li><em>\+\+ *(?:_0x(?:[a-f0-9]){4,6}|(?:\b|\d)[a-z0-9]{1,4}(?:\b|\d))</em></li>
	<li><em>while (true) {}</em></li>
	<li><em>return (function()</em></li>
	<li><em>{}.constructor(&quot;return this&quot;)( )</em></li>
	<li><em>4|0|7|5|3|1|8|2|6</em></li>
	<li><em>2|1|0|6|3|5|4</em></li>
	<li><em>split</em></li>
	<li><em>debug</em></li>
	<li><em>error</em></li>
	<li><em>exception</em></li>
	<li><em>trace</em></li>
	<li><em>http://thewomentour.com/wp-includes/f8yezb9/</em></li>
	<li><em>WScript.Shell</em></li>
	<li><em>ResponseBody</em></li>
	<li><em>ActiveXObject</em></li>
	<li><em>https://www.marquedafrique.com/k9c5qh/eb1wiw8192/</em></li>
	<li><em>Scripting.FileSystemObject</em></li>
	<li><em>CreateObject</em></li>
	<li><em>https://thecrystaltrees.com/nofij3ksa/o5523/</em></li>
	<li><em>http://4excellent.com/wp-includes/ii950106/</em></li>
	<li><em>WScript.Shell</em></li>
	<li><em>Popup</em></li>
	<li><em>MSXML2.XMLHTTP</em></li>
	<li><em>GET</em></li>
	<li><em>open</em></li>
	<li><em>send</em></li>
	<li><em>http://www.davidleighlaw.com/wp-content/wlfsj15707/</em></li>
	<li><em>Position</em></li>
	<li><em>Open</em></li>
	<li><em>Type</em></li>
	<li><em>SaveToFile</em></li>
	<li><em>random</em></li>
	<li><em>toString</em></li>
	<li><em>substr</em></li>
	<li><em>0|1|3|4|2</em></li>
	<li><em>11|15|13|4|6|9|8|7|5|0|2|3|1|10|16|14|12</em></li>
	<li><em>return (function()</em></li>
	<li><em>{}.constructor(&quot;return this&quot;)( )</em></li>
	<li><em>7|2|8|0|5|1|4|6|3</em></li>
	<li><em>2|0|3|4|1</em></li>
	<li><em>0|14|11|8|3|6|13|9|5|2|1|12|4|10|7</em></li>
	<li><em>Not Supported File Format</em></li>
	<li><em>There was an error opening this document. The file is damaged and could not be repaired (for example, it was sent as an email attachment and wasn&#x27;t correctly decoded).</em></li>
</ul>

<p>The script&#x27;s functionality can be clearly determined from the decrypted strings. It downloads, saves, and runs its payload from a list of URLs and shows the following message box to trick a user into believing the file is corrupt:</p>

<p><img alt="Error message to trick user into believing file is corrupt" src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/10%20error_message.png" style="height:254px;width:700px" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><em>Figure 13: An error message to trick a user into believing the file is corrupt.</em></p>

<p><br/>
There are multiple URLs embedded in the script files. The following URLs were extracted from this script:<br/>
<br/>
http://thewomentour[.]com/wp-includes/f8yezb9/<br/>
https://www[.]marquedafrique[.]com/k9c5qh/eb1wiw8192/<br/>
https://thecrystaltrees[.]com/nofij3ksa/o5523/<br/>
http://4excellent[.]com/wp-includes/ii950106/<br/>
http://www[.]davidleighlaw[.]com/wp-content/wlfsj15707/</p>

<p><br/>
In this case, the Emotet loader is downloaded from “http://thecrystaltrees[.]com/nofij3ksa/o5523/” (MD5 – 402b20268d64acded1c48ce760c76c47).</p>

<p>The Emotet loader already has been extensively analyzed and blogged about, so we won&#x27;t be getting into technical details of the loader here. Below are artifacts extracted from this sample:</p>

<p><strong>RSA key extracted from this sample:</strong></p>

<p>-----BEGIN PUBLIC KEY-----\nMHwwDQYJKoZIhvcNAQEBBQADawAwaAJhAOzoTryw1r9RxRJPFKalO4+q7JaDZWSB\nKZlEc22H6ITuE06tvJspue42TF1yk8xN+1bqW++QeV6Clm1uRswA/qoao/6p4eN0\nh4zIO8PEaJ0C/9EO4cx9yfRLlVpjdEkP0QIDAQAB\n-----END PUBLIC KEY-----</p>

<p>Conclusion</p>

<p>Emotet is an ever-evolving threat, employing new tricks and tactics. Although it started as a banking trojan, Emotet is now associated with several different malware campaigns, including ransomware and infostealers. The Zscaler ThreatLabZ team proactively tracks and ensures coverage to block downloaders, payloads, and C&amp;C activity from Emotet and other threats.</p>

<hr/>

common for cybercriminals to launch an attack, then shortly thereafter stop the campaign before they are detected. These breaks also give these bad actors a chance to change tactics to, once again, attempt to avoid detection. That’s what operators using the Emotet malware did, taking a short break before bringing Emotet back in a new, more dangerous form.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eEmotet operators took about a two-month break as command and control (C\u0026amp;C) servers went down in late May and came back online around the end of August. Then, we began observing a new version of this malware around mid-September.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eEmotet started as a banking trojan in 2014. However, it has morphed into a very prominent threat. Now, it is mostly used for spamming and downloading additional malware threats on a target system. Based on the unique sample count of malware threats seen by the Zscaler Cloud Sandbox, Emotet and its downloaders appear to be among the most prevalent threats in 2019, followed by banking trojans and loaders, such as TrickBot and Ursnif, remote-access trojans (RATs), and off-the-shelf password stealers, such as LokiBot and AZORult.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eEmotet is modular by design, as it supports multiple modules for different tasks, such as stealing information, spamming, and more. It is also known to download and to be downloaded by other malware families, such as TrickBot and Ursnif. It has also been associated with the Ryuk ransomware.\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eEmail conversation hijacking\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis year, Emotet employed a new tactic of using stolen email content in spam campaigns. The hijacking of existing email threads can be very effective as recipients are tricked into believing that the email was sent by the other person in the email thread. This trust factor can lead to the victim opening the email (and attachment) and getting infected with Emotet, effectively making the infected system part of an Emotet botnet.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Graph showing break in emotet activity from the beginning of June 2019 to mid september 2019\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/2%20emotet_gap.png\" style=\"height:250px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 1: Emotet activity from the beginning of June 2019 to mid-September 2019.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Emotet new campaign after break\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/3%20emotet%20after%20break.png\" style=\"height:250px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 2: The new Emotet campaign after the break.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eNew campaign, new document templates, and new botnets?\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe observed the following new templates in spammed malicious documents (maldocs) during this new campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"New Macro Template (Product Notice) \" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/4%20macro_template1.png\" style=\"float:left; height:243px; width:349px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e  \u003cimg alt=\"New Macro Template (Protected View) \" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/5%20macro_template3.png\" style=\"height:243px; width:349px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigures 3 and 4: New macro templates (Product Notice and Protected View)\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eEarlier, there were two Emotet botnets, known as Epoch 1 (E1) and Epoch2 (E2), that were using unique RSA keys to communicate with their C\u0026amp;C. After the break, we noticed three new RSA keys being used, which suggests the possibility of a botnet splitting into multiple botnets. Earlier keys were no longer seen in use and the latest three keys are now being used, which means operators are reorganizing their botnet infrastructure.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cstrong\u003eAlready existing RSA keys \u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n-----BEGIN PUBLIC break.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\nRSA1 and RSA2 were used before the break. In this new campaign, we saw Emotet using RSA3, RSA4, and RSA5. (1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 are assigned based on their first observation sequence in the wild).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBefore the break, the two RSA keys didn't share any C\u0026amp;C infrastructure. In this new campaign, two sub-botnets are sharing some infrastructure (as shown in the following screenshots).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Old RSA keys and C\u0026amp;C infrastructure\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/old_rsa_keys.png\" style=\"height:371px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 6: Emotet RSA keys and C\u0026amp;C infrastructure before the break.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"New RSA keys and C\u0026amp;C infrastructure\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/new_rsa_keys.png\" style=\"height:371px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 7: RSA keys and C\u0026amp;C infrastructure of the new Emotet campaign.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIf we check the overall C\u0026amp;C infrastructure and RSA key relationships before and after the break, we can clearly see a reorganization of the C\u0026amp;C infrastructure, which is now divided among three new Epochs. One Epoch is divided into two while the other one is used to create a single botnet with some new C\u0026amp;Cs.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"RSA key and C\u0026amp;C infrastrucutre share between before and after botnets\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/before_after_rsa_keys_shared_c2.png\" style=\"height:371px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 8: The Emotet RSA key and C\u0026amp;C infrastructure relationships before and after the break.\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eEmotet Downloader payload - Technical analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Emotet infection cycle generally starts with spam emails containing malicious macro documents that drop a JavaScript file. This JavaScript file further downloads the Emotet payload from a compromised WordPress website. Almost all the samples we observed were served from compromised WordPress websites (mostly version 5.2.3). \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWe will take a look at one such malicious document for the purpose of analysis here - \u003cbr\u003e\r\nMD5 – 359696113a2156617c28d4f79cc7d44b (“file 20190924 LTR6051.doc”)\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe macro in the documents is quite simple and straightforward but contains lots of junk.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Macro code containing junk instructions\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/6%20macro_with_junk.png\" style=\"height:327px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 9: Macro code containing junk instructions.\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter removing the junk, this is how the macro code looks.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Cleaned Macro code\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/7%20cleaned_macro.png\" style=\"height:609px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 10: Cleaned macro code.\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt gets its text from TextBox1 in UserForm2, then saves that in a \"JS\" file before executing that file.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"User form containing javascript code\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/8%20user_form.png\" style=\"height:216px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 11: A user form containing javascript code.\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\n \u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis JavaScript file is heavily obfuscated. More obfuscation is being added to the \"JS\" code incrementally. As in earlier versions of this downloader, some of the strings and function names were readable and now almost every string is obfuscated.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Heavily obfuscated script\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/9%20obfuscated_js.png\" style=\"height:476px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 12: Heavily obfuscated script\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\nThis script contains an array of strings in variable “a.” First, the elements of the array are shuffled using an anonymous function just after the array definition. Then there is function “b,” which is used to decrypt strings and is extensively used throughout the script. Using this function, we can log the decrypted strings just before they return. Some of the interesting strings include:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cem\u003e\\+\\+ The file is damaged and could not be repaired (for example, it was sent as an email attachment and wasn't correctly decoded).\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe script's functionality can be clearly determined from the decrypted strings. It downloads, saves, and runs its payload from a list of URLs and shows the following message box to trick a user into believing the file is corrupt:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"Error message to trick user into believing file is corrupt\" src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/emotet/emotet-is-back-oct-2019/10%20error_message.png\" style=\"height:254px; width:700px\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 13: An error message to trick a user into believing the file is corrupt.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\nThere are multiple URLs embedded in the script files. The following URLs were extracted from this script:\u003cbr\u003e\r\n\u003cbr\u003e\r\nhttp://thewomentour[.]com/wp-includes/f8yezb9/\u003cbr\u003e\r\nhttps://www[.]marquedafrique[.]com/k9c5qh/eb1wiw8192/\u003cbr\u003e\r\nhttps://thecrystaltrees[.]com/nofij3ksa/o5523/\u003cbr\u003e\r\nhttp://4excellent[.]com/wp-includes/ii950106/\u003cbr\u003e\r\nhttp://www[.]davidleighlaw[.]com/wp-content/wlfsj15707/\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\r\nIn this case, the Emotet loader is downloaded from “http://thecrystaltrees[.]com/nofij3ksa/o5523/” (MD5 – 402b20268d64acded1c48ce760c76c47).\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe Emotet loader already has been extensively analyzed and blogged about, so we won't be getting into technical details of the loader here. is an ever-evolving threat, employing new tricks and tactics. Although it started as a banking trojan, Emotet is now associated with several different malware campaigns, including ransomware and infostealers.
